<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_251240061">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/251240061" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5686305-shayantani-das" class="left imgcol" title="Shayantani Das"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1436246159p2/5686305.jpg" alt="Shayantani Das" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/251240061?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jan 23, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5686305-shayantani-das" class="user" title="Shayantani Das">Shayantani Das</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/17061.Coraline" title="Paperback 9780061139376" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5686305-shayantani-das?shelf=favorite-protagonist" class="actionLinkLite">favorite-protagonist</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5686305-shayantani-das?shelf=well-developed-plot" class="actionLinkLite">well-developed-plot</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer251240061">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2963890704996039553">I absolutely adore pre teen narrators. I love seeing their worlds from their POV. No melodrama, no hesitation, no wistful thinking about the hot guy/girl, no inner monologue and most importantly no gray shades. I am not saying that those are bad aspects of a story and that I don’t enjoy them (except perhaps the continuous yakking about how handsome the new guy is). Still, books from the preteen perspective always prove to be a refreshing experience. A Relaxing yet fun experience, just like this</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2963890704996039553">I absolutely adore pre teen narrators. I love seeing their worlds from their POV. No melodrama, no hesitation, no wistful thinking about the hot guy/girl, no inner monologue and most importantly no gray shades. I am not saying that those are bad aspects of a story and that I don’t enjoy them (except perhaps the continuous yakking about how handsome the new guy is). Still, books from the preteen perspective always prove to be a refreshing experience. A Relaxing yet fun experience, just like this book was.<br /><br />Flavia De Luce will always remain my favorite preteen, but Coraline definitely gets the second spot. She is different, loves exploring, wants to wear rainbow colored gloves to school and hates it when people get her name wrong (they call her Caroline, how boring!). Her parents are always glued to the computer, making poor Coraline feel lonely and bored. She also has very interesting neighbors, like the actresses below with their dozens of dogs, or he mad man upstairs trying to train rats to dance and sing. <br /><br />On one fateful rainy day, poor Coraline is confined to her house by her mother. So our little explorer decides to explore the house and comes across this peculiar door opening to ….nothing but a brick wall. Unfortunately, (fortunate for us) there is more to it than that. The tiny door in her house sometimes leads her to a place exactly like her original house, only it’s perfect. It also has her parents, and they are perfect too, with her mother making delicious food and her father spending quality time with her. Something is amiss though, they have buttons sewed instead of eyes and calls themselves Coraline’s “other mother “and “other father”. Soon, Coraline realizes that she has chosen an adventure which is darker and more sinister than she earlier imagined.<br /><br />Along with being an awesome adventure, the book focuses on Coraline’s personality development through the novel. She doesn’t gets all mature and serious (God gracious no! how dreadful would that be!) but finds courage and wisdom within her which she didn’t know existed. She uses her wit to save herself and her parents, takes steps which are very hard for her and gives us some memorable quotes like these:<br /><br /><em>“I don't want whatever I want. Nobody does. Not really. What kind of fun would it be if I just got everything I ever wanted just like that, and it didn't mean anything? What then?”</em><br /><br />And <br /><br /><em>“Because,' she said, 'when you're scared but you still do it anyway, that's brave” i&gt;<br /><br />Also, some other interesting characters come along. Like, the cat (cat’s don’t have a name you see); now if only cats were so witty in real life, and could talk :(. There are also her neighbors, and the ancient souls. On a whole, this book felt like a modern version of Alice in the Wonderland. It was jolly fun.<br /><br />An extra charm was added to this already amazing book by Neil Gaiman’s voice <em>*deep sigh*. </em> I swear I can listen to the man all day long, even if all he said was yada yada. He doesn’t, hopefully, and is the best narrator anyone can ever hope for.<br /><br /><strong> so sparking 4.5 stars from me, and an extra unwanted advice of listening to the audio book version. </strong> :D<br /></em></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="2963890704996039553">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1195001">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1195001" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="left imgcol" title="J.G. Keely"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p2/84023.jpg" alt="J.G. Keely" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1195001?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" class="user" title="J.G. Keely">J.G. Keely</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/17061.Coraline" title="Paperback 9780061139376" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=illustrated" class="actionLinkLite">illustrated</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/84023-j-g-keely?shelf=uk-and-ireland" class="actionLinkLite">uk-and-ireland</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1195001">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3355585572388832312">I actually picked up a signed copy at a local shop when Gaiman and I were both living in Minnesota. I've read a lot of Gaiman, and while appreciate that he is always a conscious, active writer, his stories sometimes fall flat for me. <br /><br />They always work on the principle of a small person trapped in a large, unknown world. There are plenty of great examples of this story type, and Gaiman has been steadily working through them. He took inspiration from Fairy Tales in Stardust, from European myth in A</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3355585572388832312">I actually picked up a signed copy at a local shop when Gaiman and I were both living in Minnesota. I've read a lot of Gaiman, and while appreciate that he is always a conscious, active writer, his stories sometimes fall flat for me. <br /><br />They always work on the principle of a small person trapped in a large, unknown world. There are plenty of great examples of this story type, and Gaiman has been steadily working through them. He took inspiration from Fairy Tales in Stardust, from European myth in American Gods, and African myth in Anansi Boys. Though Morpheus was no small man, the individual story arcs dealt with normal folks. Sandman and Good Omens worked off of Christian mythos, while Neverwhere created myths from modern symbols.<br /><br />If Neverwhere is a rewrite of Alice in Wonderland, Coraline is in some ways an even closer take on Carroll, except that here, Gaiman is exploring a more overtly frightening world, evoking Gothic ghost stories. Unlike his other stories, Gaiman has less to draw on here. He is not bringing in specific myths, but rather creating his own symbols. Since he is not bringing in the many and varied elements that mark most of his tales, Coraline sets a much barer stage.<br /><br />When he does bring in mythological elements, he always put his own spin on them, so he cannot be faulted for a lack of creative force. Indeed, he is at his most engaging when he is exploring and subverting various world mythologies, of which he is well-versed.<br /><br />Even in the less mythologized Neverwhere, he drew on the visual imagery and history of London itself, a great city which traces its roots from before Rome, and is not without its own legends. By eschewing any particular tradition in Coraline, Gaiman has little to play with. He has nothing to subvert, nothing to vaguely reference. He cannot play upon our expectations.<br /><br />All this tracks back to the reason that Gaiman explores these mythologies in the first place: his interest in writing about storytelling itself. Each time he writes, he places himself in a tradition, recognizing how the ancients used myth and symbol to create stories that instruct, inspire, and surprise.<br /><br />Coraline does not explore its own origins. It does not display the genre savvy play of Gaiman's other work. It is not an exploration of the ghost story, nor of 'Through the Looking-Glass'. It is not a deconstruction of the Gothic.<br /><br />It is a simple little tale, and not without its charm. I found little frightening about it, simply because there was little that was either unexpected or psychologically gripping. The most interesting element was the way he played with how we learn about identity.<br /><br />There is a point in childhood when we suspect that there us something that makes up identity beyond simply the appearance or form. The idea that a parent or friend could be replaced by a doppelganger is inherently terrifying. However, Gaiman does not produce a new twist on doppelgangers or changelings.<br /><br />Neither did the portrayal utilize surprise or subtlety to develop an unsettling mood. Rather, he presented overtly frightening or alien elements, bolstered by the characters' reactions.<br /><br />But it's not frightening to simply show scary things. Hearing a strange noise in the woods is not the same as being told that a character hears a strange noise in the woods. It only becomes frightening when the vividness of the description or the realism of the character's psychology allows us to tap into that sense of fear.<br /><br />This little story could have made a passable entry in a horror story collection, but is not original enough to stand on its own. I found this rather odd, since Gaiman is entirely capable of creating frightening stories, as evidenced by the fairy tale rewrite 'Snow, Glass, Apples' (from 'Smoke and Mirrors').<br /><br />He has been frightening, disturbing, and unusual elsewhere, but here, I found little to speak of his creative flair.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3355585572388832312">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div class="brown_comment" id="comment_140657959">
  <div class="xhr_comment_photo">
    	<a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" alt="J.G. Keely"><img alt="84023" src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1274333636p1/84023.jpg" /></a>
  </div>
  <div class="xhr_comment_body">
    <a href="/user/show/84023-j-g-keely" style="font-weight:bold">J.G. Keely</a>

      
<span id="freeTextContainer9615659894789630948"><em>Outis said: "Pop culture can be scary if you're not used to it."</em><br /><br />Hell, even when you are used to it, it can still be pretty unsettling.<br /><br />But yeah, part o</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9615659894789630948"><em>Outis said: "Pop culture can be scary if you're not used to it."</em><br /><br />Hell, even when you are used to it, it can still be pretty unsettling.<br /><br />But yeah, part of what intrigues me is that, rather than focusing on how to communicate effectively with children, how to provide context for their experiences in the world, these arguments often focus on trying to shut the child away and insulate them.<br /><br />As Rocko pointed out, this often seems to have more to do with making the parents' job easier rather than thinking about how to introduce and explain things to the child--that the real problem is not that there is any psychological damage to the child, but that the parent is now forced to watch them explore and play out scenarios which the parent finds bothersome or embarrassing, and which the parent would rather not have to explain to them.<br /><br /><em>"I don't think my parents sent me messages along those lines. They simply didn't expose me to the TV programs and movies ... because they weren't into pop culture"</em><br /><br />But isn't that also a message? Aren't they providing judgments and assumptions about how to interact with culture? Also, if your parents weren't deliberately insulating you from such 'bad TV', do you mean to say that you were the rare incurious child who didn't try to push boundaries and explore things on their own?</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9615659894789630948">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4944698280964749895">Without computer networks, video tapes, cable TV or the money for the movie theater my ability to explore the world of moving images was very limited</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4944698280964749895">Without computer networks, video tapes, cable TV or the money for the movie theater my ability to explore the world of moving images was very limited indeed. And I remember moving images as being a lot scarier than drawings or the written word.<br />I don't remember anyone telling me that some TV was "bad" but if I had access to a TV, it was only during hours at which very tame programs were shown. The stuff I found scary I only saw when I met kids whose relatives could afford some kind of video tape player.</span>

          <a href="/book/show/17061.Coraline" title="Paperback 9780061139376" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=the-movie-is-better" class="actionLinkLite">the-movie-is-better</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/111921-madeline?shelf=kids-and-young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">kids-and-young-adult</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer192260630">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16860650933252837463">A fun, quick read. I like to think of this book as Gaiman's response to the Narnia books - you kids want to escape your boring normal lives? Well, you're in for a surprise, because fantasy worlds are scary as fuck. Sleep well! <br /><br />The movie version, with Dakota Fanning voicing Coraline, is not technically "better" than the book (as one of my shelf titles would have you believe). Instead, the movie actually enhances Gaiman's already-great story, with appropriately-haunting music, gorgeous animation,</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText16860650933252837463">A fun, quick read. I like to think of this book as Gaiman's response to the Narnia books - you kids want to escape your boring normal lives? Well, you're in for a surprise, because fantasy worlds are scary as fuck. Sleep well! <br /><br />The movie version, with Dakota Fanning voicing Coraline, is not technically "better" than the book (as one of my shelf titles would have you believe). Instead, the movie actually enhances Gaiman's already-great story, with appropriately-haunting music, gorgeous animation, and some nice extra stuff added in, like Coraline's friend Wybie and the holy-shit-get-it-away doll that the Other Mother uses to spy on kids. Worth seeing, and worth reading. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="16860650933252837463">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/113564428?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 04, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1742824-jason-koivu" class="user" title="Jason Koivu">Jason Koivu</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1742824-jason-koivu?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer113564428">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18380572237035176999">A bit austere in the detail department, but pretty creepy for a kids book. I listened to the audio version with Gaiman as the narrator. On the one hand, I'm wary of authors reading their own work only from the standpoint that they are not seasoned reading veterans. On the other hand, they approach the narration fully armed with the knowledge of what was meant by each line, what emphasis should be placed where and how each line of dialogue should be delivered. Gaiman is an adept reader, so listen</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18380572237035176999">A bit austere in the detail department, but pretty creepy for a kids book. I listened to the audio version with Gaiman as the narrator. On the one hand, I'm wary of authors reading their own work only from the standpoint that they are not seasoned reading veterans. On the other hand, they approach the narration fully armed with the knowledge of what was meant by each line, what emphasis should be placed where and how each line of dialogue should be delivered. Gaiman is an adept reader, so listening to him read <em>Coraline</em> was a pleasure. The audiobook also included music by one of my favorite musical artists, Stephin Merritt (Magnetic Fields, 6ths, Gothic Archies, etc) who also did work for the <em>Series of Unfortunate Events</em> books by his friend Daniel Handler, aka Lemony Snicket.</span>

  <a href="/user/show/22313662-laz" class="left imgcol" title="Laz"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1445857941p2/22313662.jpg" alt="Laz" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/986662399?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 23, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/22313662-laz" class="user" title="Laz">Laz</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/17061.Coraline" title="Paperback 9780061139376" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">fairy tale lovers</span></div>

        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=horror" class="actionLinkLite">horror</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=fairy-tales" class="actionLinkLite">fairy-tales</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/22313662-laz?shelf=all-time-favorites" class="actionLinkLite">all-time-favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer986662399">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6781424169889789987"><strong>This is a fascinating and stunning book. Or fairy tale.</strong> Call it whatever you like. For me it was more of a fairy tale because it's adventurous and it travelled me to a very bizzare world which most children would find fascinating but adults would freak the hell out. I'll forever consider myself a kid at heart even though I am 19, so, this book, this masterpiece, it's a fairy tale to me.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1414254564i/11630302._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Coraline is a small girl, petite if you'd like, who finds herself tangled up in a very dark and mysterious stor</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6781424169889789987"><strong>This is a fascinating and stunning book. Or fairy tale.</strong> Call it whatever you like. For me it was more of a fairy tale because it's adventurous and it travelled me to a very bizzare world which most children would find fascinating but adults would freak the hell out. I'll forever consider myself a kid at heart even though I am 19, so, this book, this masterpiece, it's a fairy tale to me.<br /><br /><img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1414254564i/11630302._SY540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Coraline is a small girl, petite if you'd like, who finds herself tangled up in a very dark and mysterious story. She comes face to face with her other mother, a being who's bleak and devours children for breakfast. She says she loves Coraline, but this kind of love is how cats love to eat mice. There were other children before her, and now they're all trapped inside a mirror forever. Coraline strikes a deal with her other mother and it's her only way out.<br /><br />What I loved about this fairy tale it's the fact that the heroine isn't just a child. She's a wise child, she is not ignorant. She's quick-to-like and she's an adventurous kid with lots of love for exploring. If it was anyone else, other than her, I don't really believe she would have managed to come out of this unscathed. <br /><br />By the by, I'll post a quote below which grew quickly to become one of my favorite lines ever:<br /><br /><u>"But how can you walk away from something and still come back to it?"<br />"Easy," said the cat. "Think of somebody walking around the world. You start out walking away from something and end up coming back to it."<br />"Small world," said Coraline."</u></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6781424169889789987">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_27363859">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/27363859" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/424766-karen" class="left imgcol" title="Karen"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1208220441p2/424766.jpg" alt="Karen" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/27363859?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Aug 17, 2008</a>

      <a href="/user/show/424766-karen" class="user" title="Karen">Karen</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok">it was ok</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="it was ok"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/589836.Coraline" title="Paperback 9780380807345" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/424766-karen?shelf=fiction" class="actionLinkLite">fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/424766-karen?shelf=childrens" class="actionLinkLite">childrens</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/424766-karen?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer27363859">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10264544074492581993">I'm a little confused by this book. On the one hand, I found the story compelling. I read it very quickly because the story was quite gripping. On the other hand, I was puzzled by it. On the cover, one of the reviewers compares the book to the Narnia books. However, those books were wonderful allegories. I cannot see any allegorical value in this story. <br /><br />In the book, a little girl who lives in an apartment with her parents is intrigued by a locked door that opens to the bricked off entrance of th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText10264544074492581993">I'm a little confused by this book. On the one hand, I found the story compelling. I read it very quickly because the story was quite gripping. On the other hand, I was puzzled by it. On the cover, one of the reviewers compares the book to the Narnia books. However, those books were wonderful allegories. I cannot see any allegorical value in this story. <br /><br />In the book, a little girl who lives in an apartment with her parents is intrigued by a locked door that opens to the bricked off entrance of the empty apartment next door. One night, she opens the door to find the bricks gone. The door now opens to a dark hallway that leads into an apartment that is just like hers but is very different and is where her "other" mother and "other" father live. They want her to stay with them forever and always. Without giving away too much, the other world through the door is a frightening mockery of her real world. The "other" mother becomes the evil conflict in this story. But why is the "other" mother evil? Who is she? Why is she doing this? It's unsettling and full of uncertainty. <br /><br />The book cover also says this book is for children eight years of age and older. I'm not sure what an eight-year-old would make of this story. It would be quite scary and alarming for an eight-year-old child. <br /><br />In the end, good triumphs but triumphing over what is the part of the story that stumps me. Yes, I suppose, triumphing over evil, but the reason for the evil is unclear. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="10264544074492581993">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_36063394">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36063394" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1219253-amanda" class="left imgcol" title="Amanda"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1411956191p2/1219253.jpg" alt="Amanda" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/36063394?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 22, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1219253-amanda" class="user" title="Amanda">Amanda</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1219253-amanda?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1219253-amanda?shelf=blog" class="actionLinkLite">blog</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer36063394">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7547214087750168077">Creepy, quirky, and all things to be expected of a Gaiman novel, this is one of those books that I wish had been available to me as a young 10 to 12 year old when all I had to fall back on was the Nancy Drew series. There was certainly nothing this twisted and delightfully dark on the shelves of the school library. As a heroine, Coraline is a likable, brave girl who takes matters into her own hands when her parents are kidnapped by a dark force that should scare her shitless. Young girls, force-</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText7547214087750168077">Creepy, quirky, and all things to be expected of a Gaiman novel, this is one of those books that I wish had been available to me as a young 10 to 12 year old when all I had to fall back on was the Nancy Drew series. There was certainly nothing this twisted and delightfully dark on the shelves of the school library. As a heroine, Coraline is a likable, brave girl who takes matters into her own hands when her parents are kidnapped by a dark force that should scare her shitless. Young girls, force-fed Barbie worship and <em>Twilight</em>-y longing for an undead mate, need books like these because, frankly, that much exposure to all things pink and sparkly eventually gets boring. I also like that Gaiman sidesteps the whole "it was just a dream" scenario and ends with enough evidence that Coraline's experience was just what it was--a daunting quest undertaken by an undaunted little girl.<br /><br />Cross posted at <a rel="nofollow" href="http://viiamanda.blogspot.com/">This Insignificant Cinder</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="7547214087750168077">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer598297525420292357">YAY! I LOVED THIS! :D<br /><br />So, basically, I can now safely say that Neil Gaiman 1/ Is in my list of favourite authors, and 2/ has one of the most beautiful voices I've heard.<br /><br />I will be doing a review of this on my YouTube Channel, but here are some thoughts:<br /><br />1) This was genuinely creepy and morbid at times, and when the mice sang their voice actors were so creepy I honestly felt fear.<br />2) I love how straight forward and simplistic this book was. This can be attributed to the fact that it's a children's bo</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText598297525420292357">YAY! I LOVED THIS! :D<br /><br />So, basically, I can now safely say that Neil Gaiman 1/ Is in my list of favourite authors, and 2/ has one of the most beautiful voices I've heard.<br /><br />I will be doing a review of this on my YouTube Channel, but here are some thoughts:<br /><br />1) This was genuinely creepy and morbid at times, and when the mice sang their voice actors were so creepy I honestly felt fear.<br />2) I love how straight forward and simplistic this book was. This can be attributed to the fact that it's a children's book, but The Ocean at the End of the Lane was also quite simplistic and so I think it's a Neil Gaiman thing and I love it.<br />3) I really liked Coraline as a main character: she was down to earth, shy, independent, and brave even when she was scared.<br />4) Neil Gaiman can create ambiance like nobody's business.<br />5) I can't wait to read my next Neil Gaiman: The Graveyard Book!</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_349510887">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/349510887" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/9374686-clouds" class="left imgcol" title="Clouds"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1423860896p2/9374686.jpg" alt="Clouds" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/349510887?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 30, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9374686-clouds" class="user" title="Clouds">Clouds</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/842927.Coraline" title="Paperback 9780747562108" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=fantasy-stand-alone" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-stand-alone</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=locus-ya" class="actionLinkLite">locus-ya</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=saw-the-movie" class="actionLinkLite">saw-the-movie</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=young-adult" class="actionLinkLite">young-adult</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=read-in-2013" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2013</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9374686-clouds?shelf=pub-2000s" class="actionLinkLite">pub-2000s</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer349510887">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11554256777119624133"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.theaceofclouds.com/"> <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif" /></a><br /><strong>Following the resounding success of my <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2">Locus Quest</a>, I faced a dilemma: which reading list to follow it up with? Variety is the spice of life, so I’ve decided to diversify and pursue six different lists simultaneously. This book falls into my <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=4">LOCUS Y-A</a> list.<br /><br />I think I’ll always have a soft-spot for imaginative young-adult speculative fiction and as the good people at Locus did such a grand job with picking their Sci-Fi winners, I’ll trust them to single out some special y-a books too.</strong><br /><br />When I pulle</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11554256777119624133"><a rel="nofollow" href="http://www.theaceofclouds.com/">
  <img class="escapedImg" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1380317319i/689370.gif" /></a><br /><strong>Following the resounding success of my <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=2">Locus Quest</a>, I faced a dilemma: which reading list to follow it up with? Variety is the spice of life, so I’ve decided to diversify and pursue six different lists simultaneously. This book falls into my <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/story/show/332414-reading-lists?chapter=4">LOCUS Y-A</a> list.<br /><br />I think I’ll always have a soft-spot for imaginative young-adult speculative fiction and as the good people at Locus did such a grand job with picking their Sci-Fi winners, I’ll trust them to single out some special y-a books too.</strong><br /><br />When I pulled up the list of previous Locus Y-A Award winners, I wasn’t surprised to see <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/842927.Coraline">
  <strong>
    <em>Coraline</em>
  </strong>
</a> taking the 2003 prize. It’s one of those books that I had been meaning to get around to reading since it came out but never quite got to before the film came out in 2009. I probably would have picked it up sooner, if it wasn’t for the huge success of the film - I know that may sound counter-intuitive, but with the movie still fresh in my mind, I didn’t want to go into the book with my imagination pre-programmed.<br /><br />Having left it a few years, it was now possible to read the book on its own terms, without hearing Dakota Fanning’s voice coming out of Coraline’s mouth or picturing the world through Selick’s stop-motion interpretation. And, golly-gee, it was worth the wait!<br /><br />I think what grabbed me first was the scarcity of the descriptions. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman">Gaiman</a> does a marvellous job of conveying much while saying little. It would have been easy to tunnel down into many of these moments to build depth, tension and atmosphere, etc – in fact, that’s normally one thing I love a good writer doing – but <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/842927.Coraline">
  <strong>
    <em>Coraline</em>
  </strong>
</a> is the work of a master storyteller. Reading it is like catching in the distance the melody of a complex pop song played on pan-pipes – it’s been stripped down to the bare essentials, but remains just as likely to get stuck in your head and your own mind does all the extra imaginative work, fleshing out the skeleton.<br /><br />Having said all that, the little details that <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman">Gaiman</a> does embellish his tale with are vivid and evocative, imbuing his titular character and her world their own clear and quirky style. Much like a few eye catching pieces of jewellery (or some dayglo green gloves and wellington boots that look like frogs) can really set-off an outfit.<br /><br />Coraline herself is a wonderful heroine and I’ve come to the conclusion that her attitude is perfect for this story. She is so very matter-of-fact and quietly determined, in complete contrast to the dark whimsy of the other-world. In my review of <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2213661.The_Graveyard_Book">
  <em>The Graveyard Book</em>
</a> (which is a cut from similar cloth) my only complaint was that the hero of that story, a young boy called Nobody Jones, was a too much an ‘everyman’ character, easily identified with but lacking a bit of sparkle himself. I was tempted to make the same complaint in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/842927.Coraline">
  <strong>
    <em>Coraline</em>
  </strong>
</a> – surely a girl with a bit of sass would have captured my heart more? I’m a sucker for a sassy girl. But on reflection, I’ve decided that Coraline is just right the way she is. If she’d had more fun with her adventure – treated it like playtime, like a complicated game – it would have lost a lot of its weight. But Coraline is serious, stubborn and independent, she’s not fazed by the threat the other-mother poses, but she takes it at face value and deals with it on its own terms. That grounded, sober perspective keeps the story firmly balanced, and curtails any risk of it drifting into the talking-animals and fairytale-baddies children’s fantasy pigeonhole.<br /><br />While we’re mentioning talking animals though – both the cat and the rats were highlights for me! I have three cats and I’m sure <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman">Gaiman</a> must a lot of experience with cats, because he’s got the cat-itude spot-on. <br /><br />I looked at that last line and thought, this is the era of the internet – all the information of the world is at my fingertips! So I Googled “<em>does Neil Gaiman have a cat?</em>”, found my way to the “cats” tag on his blog and fished out this quote:<blockquote>“There used to be seven cats in this house. There were always seven cats. As one died off or went walkabout, never to return, another would turn up at the back door.”</blockquote>Yes, this man knows his cats! And it shows. And I loved every scene involving the-cat -with-no-name. Here are a couple of quotes, because they are smile-worthy.<blockquote>“What's your name,' Coraline asked the cat. 'Look, I'm Coraline. Okay?'<br />'Cats don't have names,' it said.<br />'No?' said Coraline.<br />'No,' said the cat. 'Now you people have names. That's because you don't know who you are. We know who we are, so we don't need names.”</blockquote><blockquote>“The cat wrinkled its nose and managed to look unimpressed. "Calling cats," it confided, "tends to be a rather overrated activity. Might as well call a whirlwind.”</blockquote>And then there are the rats. I’ve read a few books recently with talking rats. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5659.The_Wind_in_the_Willows">
  <em>Wind in the Willows</em>
</a>, with good old Ratty. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/474794.Thomas">
  <em>Thomas</em>
</a>, with its evil (but stupid) minion rats. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34534.The_Amazing_Maurice_and_His_Educated_Rodents">
  <em>The Amazing Maurice</em>
</a>, with its brave, magic <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/series/40650-discworld">Discworld</a> rats (go, go, Dangerous Beans!). All three of these present their rats as small, furry people - but <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/842927.Coraline">
  <strong>
    <em>Coraline</em>
  </strong>
</a> does not. The rats are something like an elemental evil. Like the spirit of treachery embodied. Or the essence of malignancy made flesh. They’re genuinely creepy!<br /><br />Which brings us nicely on to the buttons: will I ever look at buttons the same way? I think not. There is a beads and buttons shop in the Cardiff arcades, and every time I walk past I see the ghosts of a thousand other-mothers in their window display. Best subversion of a standard household object ever. Come here Coraline, so I can sew buttons into your eyes and you can stay and love me forever! Yuk, yuk, yuk! Creepy as cockroaches under your skin. Pure ick.<br /><br />Keely makes a good point about <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1221698.Neil_Gaiman">Gaiman</a>’s body of work as existing as a modern reimagining of classic myth types. <blockquote>“He took inspiration from Fairy Tales in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/16793.Stardust"><em>Stardust</em></a>, from European myth in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4407.American_Gods"><em>American Gods</em></a>, and African myth in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/2744.Anansi_Boys%22%22"><em>Anansi Boys</em></a>. Though Morpheus was no small man, the individual story arcs dealt with normal folks. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/series/40372-the-sandman"><em>Sandman</em></a> and <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/12067.Good_Omens"><em>Good Omens</em></a> worked off of Christian mythos, while <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/14497.Neverwhere"><em>Neverwhere</em></a> created myths from modern symbols”</blockquote>I (obviously) disagree with Keely’s conclusion - that <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/842927.Coraline">
  <strong>
    <em>Coraline</em>
  </strong>
</a>, standing outside of an obvious storytelling tradition is worse off for lacking the genre savvy play of his other work. <br /><br />I’ve been thinking about how I’d classify the inspiration behind <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/842927.Coraline">
  <strong>
    <em>Coraline</em>
  </strong>
</a>, and the phrase that’s come to mind is “domestic horror”. When you’re little, your parents warn you about the dangers of life. Don’t run with scissors. Don’t play with matches. Don’t take sweets from stranger. <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/author/show/1654.Terry_Pratchett">Pratchett</a> riffed off the fears these warnings can create in <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34532.Hogfather">
  <em>Hogfather</em>
</a> (the thumb-suckers thumb-cutting ostrich-scissor-monster?) – and it’s that same riff I feel running through <a href="http://www.goodreads.com/book/show/842927.Coraline">
  <strong>
    <em>Coraline</em>
  </strong>
</a>. Don’t play with the sewing box, you might hurt yourself. Don’t play with rats, they might bite. Don’t wish for another mother or your other-mother will sew buttons into your eyes.<br /><br />Or perhaps the moral is for the parents? Ignore your child too much and she may just run away to someone more attentive... who may take advantage and hurt her. Try and ignore that thought when you wave your kids away to ‘go play’ because you’re too busy, huh?<br /><br />It’s a cracking little book. So why only four stars? Because it’s teeny. It’s barely a novella. To get a full five stars I need something I can sink my teeth into. This was delicious, but I was still hungry when I finished.	<br /><br /><strong>After this I read: </strong><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/532929113">Qualia</a></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11554256777119624133">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

Wondrous gifts: the ability--with a glance, a gesture, a word--to summon animals, bring forth fire, move the land. Fearsome gifts: They can twist a limb, chain a mind, inflict a wasting illness. The Uplanders live in constant fear that one family might unleash its gift against another. Two young people, friends since childhood, decide not to use their gifts. One, a girl, refuses to bring animals to their death in the hunt. The other, a boy, wears a blindfold lest his eyes and his anger kill.In this beautifully crafted story, Ursula K. Le Guin writes of the proud cruelty of power, of how hard it is to grow up, and of how much harder still it is to find, in the world\'s darkness, gifts of light.Includes a reader\'s guide and a sample
